Preface

of Master of Science at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU).
The work has been carried out at the Section for Fluid Mechanics, Coastal
and Maritime Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark. The work
has been supervised by Professor Harry B. Bingham (DTU), Senior Researcher

==PAGE:6==
ii
==PAGE:7==
Executive Summary
The objective of this work has been to conduct a comprehensive validation of the
applicability of the Design Tool developed by (Nielsen, 2015), which estimates
the wave added resistance of ship with high block coeﬃcients, e.g. tankers and
bulkers. Based on the drawn conclusions from the validation, improvements are
implemented into the Design Tool such that the tool can predict the wave added
resistance of ships with a low block coeﬃcient i.e. container ship, RoPax etc..
The Design Tool is intended for naval architects to use during early design
stages and for rule makers in the evaluation process for how to best satisfy
environmental requirements and regulations.
The original Design Tool (Nielsen, 2015) has been re-constructed based on Froude
numbers from Fn = 0.00 to 0.25 with a step size of 0.05. Five ships, of two
bulk carriers and three tankers (reference geometries), was scaled linearly to four
combinations of length-to-breadth ratio (5 ≤Lpp/B ≤7) and breadth-to-draught
ratio (2 ≤B/TD ≤3.2), which correspond to ratios representing the world ﬂeet
of tankers and bulkers. This yields 20 scaling combination.
The wave added resistance transfer functions are calculated for each of the 20
geometries for the six Froude numbers and ten wave headings, evenly spaced
from head to beam sea (180◦≥β ≥90◦), using a number of evenly spaced
non-dimensional wave frequencies (0.6 ≤ω
p
Lpp/g ≤10). Following to beam
seas (0◦≤β ≤90◦) are taken to be zero in scope of this study.
At each Froude number and wave heading combination a wave added resistance
transfer function is calculated, where an intersection point in the high frequency
range between one curve calculated by strip theory (Salvesen, 1978) and the
asymptotic limit (Faltinsen et al., 1980) is found creating one curve from the
two methods. Then, the ﬁve transfer functions are average to produce one curve.
==PAGE:8==
iv
These ﬁnal 120 transfer functions are saved in a database. Linear interpolation
based in the four input parameters (Lpp/B, B/TD, Fn, β) is done to predict
the wave added resistance for any given input parameters.
An extensive validation of the applicability of the original Design Tool has been
performed by comparing the wave added resistance transfer functions predicted
by the Design Tool with direct calculations using the same theoretical methodol-
ogy.
The original Design Tool showed inaccurate predictions of the wave added resis-
tance for hull geometries with Lpp/B-ratio or B/TD-ratio, or both, lying outside
the values bracketing the database of wave added transfer functions. Prediction
of geometries with Lpp/B-ratio and B/TD-ratio, but with a block coeﬃcient
signiﬁcantly less than the range spanned by the ﬁve reference geometries, showed
reasonable results.
The original Design Tool was improved by extending the range of Lpp/B-ratio
(= 4.0, 6.0, 8.0), B/TD-ratio (= 2.0, 3.5, 5.0), which enables the improved Design
Tool to predict the wave added resistance for a larger variety of ship types e.g.
container ships and RoRo ferries. Moreover, the block coeﬃcient is introduced
as an additional input parameter. The improved Design Tool is build based
on the same computational methodology as the original Design Tool, though
the wave added resistance for zero forward ship speed is substituted by results
from WAMIT. The implementation of WAMIT is due to the inaccuracy the applied
method at zero forward speed.
The improved Design Tool was comprehensively validated by applying it to eight
diﬀerent hull geometries, using the aforementioned approach. The improved
Design Tool showed good predictions of the wave added resistance for the
analyzed geometries. The average discrepancy of the peak value was 35%, which
is considered reasonably within the accuracy.
Moreover, the physical credibility of the improved Design Tool were validated by
comparing wave added resistance predictions from the tool with model test results
for one container ship, one VLCC, and one tanker type hull. A good agreement
was seen for the DTC container ship at U = 16knots and the KVLCC2 tanker
type hull at U = 15.5knots, both in head waves β = 180◦. For the ULYSSES
2020 concept hull tanker type reasonable accuracy was seen for head and bow
quartering waves (β = 180◦, 150◦) at low speeds U = 5knots, though increased
accuracy was observed for increased speed U = 10, 14knots.
An improvement of the original Design Tool has been achieved resulting in a
Design Tool, which predicts the wave added resistance with good accuracy for a
large variety of ship types (e.g. tankers, container ships etc.) based solely on the
three main dimensions and the block coeﬃcient. The improved Design Tool has
been implemented into an Excel workbook and a MATLAB package, which have
==PAGE:9==
v
been made publicly available.

Chapter 1
Introduction
1.1
Overview and Background
The shipping industry has in the recent years been exposed to an increased
environmental awareness from regulators, historically high fuel prices, and a
current over-capacity in the global shipping-ﬂeet operating on a subdued market,
which all together has created strong incentives for signiﬁcant reduction in the
operational speed of cargo ships.
Recently the Marine Environmental Protection Committee (MEPC) of the In-
ternational Maritime Organization (IMO) has adopted mandatory measures
to ensure energy eﬃciency of ships (resolution MEPC.203(62)) in terms of the
Energy Eﬃciency Design Index (EEDI). This is intended to reduce CO2 emission
from international shipping.
The EEDI expresses the ship’s environmental impact versus its beneﬁcial contri-
bution to society i.e. gram of CO2 emitted into the atmosphere per ton-nautical
mile of transported goods. Since the introduction in 2012 a further reduction of
the EEDI followed at 2015, henceforth every 5 year towards 2025. The EEDI
can in its basic form be expressed by:
EEDI = Installed Power × SFOC × fCO2
Deadweight × Speed
(1.1)
==PAGE:16==
2
Introduction
The installed power covers main and auxiliary engine power used at sea for
propulsion and electricity, SFOC is the engine speciﬁc fuel oil consumption, fCO2
is a conversion factor between fuel type consumed in tons and emitted CO2 in
tons, the deadweight is the deadweight tonnage (how much mass the ship can
safely carry excluding its own weight), and the speed is the forward ship speed
at 75% maximum continuous rating (MCR) main engine load during sea trial
conditions i.e. calm water condition.
For particular ship types, especially bulk carriers and tankers, of a certain
capacity and fuel type, to cope with the EEDI requirements, the best means are
to reduce the installed power and operational speed. Two practical approaches,
one more costly than the other, to this issue can be utilized. 1) Is to optimization
of the hull and propeller design or installation of wind or solar powered auxiliary
propulsion systems, so that an increased design speed is achieved with no changes
to the installed propulsion power or the original design speed is achieved with
less installed power. 2) Is to reduced the design speed achieving less required
power, which is easily implemented. A reduction in the EEDI is achieved, since
the power varies as a higher power of the speed. Hence, the latter being the
main approach seen in the shipping industry, this excites strong concerns about
the safety of the ship. There are limitations to the extent of power reduction
before the propulsive power reserves available onboard a ship becomes so small,
that the ship loses its ability to escape a lee shore in adverse weather.
The MARPOL Annex VI guidelines (IMO, 2013) processes these challenges
and provide guidance on how suﬃcient power reserves are maintained for ships
in adverse weather conditions. The shipping industry is pinned between two
somewhat conﬂicting regulations. On one side the cost-eﬀective solution to the
enhanced EEDI requirements, solved by reducing engine power, and on the other
side maintaining a minimum navigational speed of no less than 4 knots in adverse
weather and maintaining safe operation of the ship.
The amount of reserved power required for a ship advancing in adverse weather
conditions to keep a navigational speed of 4 knots is mainly deﬁned as a function
of the wave added resistance. In such conditions the wave added resistance
constitutes a larger part of the total resistance than the calm water resistance
contribution. However, the wave added resistance is generally not considered
in details at early stages in traditional ship design matters. A simple service
allowance of 15% - 30% is added to the calm water power curves to ensure reserve
power for adverse weather conditions (Pedersen and Aage, 2008). This approach
might be suﬃcient when designing ships with a high design speed, however it
is problematic for ships with a low design speed (slow steaming) and in the
pursuance of EEDI compliance.
The rule makers and regulators have recognized this critical issue, and through
==PAGE:17==
1.2Objectives and Scope of Thesis Work
3
the EU-FP7 funded project, Energy Eﬃcient Safe Ship Operation (SHOPERA),
the challenges are approached by looking holistically at the integrated ship design
and operational environments, and implementing optimization procedures to
optimize ships’ powering while ensuring safe ship operation.
Contributing to the challenges addressed in the SHOPERA project, Christian
Simon Nielsen developed a design tool for estimating the wave added resistance
of ships with high block coeﬃcient (Nielsen, 2015) providing solely the ships
three main dimensions as input parameter.
This study will take its origin in the Design Tool (referred to as the original
Design Tool) developed by (Nielsen, 2015). A comprehensive validation of its
applicability is performed and improvements to the original Design Tool are
implemented. Moreover, the tool is extended in the sense, that it predicts the
wave added resistance with reasonable accuracy for a larger variety of ship hull
geometries such as container ships with low block coeﬃcients.
1.2
Objectives and Scope of Thesis Work
The main objective of this work is to conduct a comprehensive validation of the
applicability of the Design Tool developed by (Nielsen, 2015), which estimates the
wave added resistance of ships with high block coeﬃcient providing solely main
dimensions. Based on the drawn conclusions from the validation, improvements
are implemented into the Design Tool, such that the tool can predict the wave
added resistance of a larger diversity of ships with low block coeﬃcient, e.g.
container ships and RoPo ship etc.. The improved Design Tool is then published
as a design tool package useful for naval architects to use during early design
stages and for rule makers in the evaluation process on how to best satisfy
environmental requirements and regulations.
The original Design Tool is re-constructed based on the methodology developed
by (Nielsen, 2015) for Froude numbers from Fn = 0.00 to 0.25 with a step size
of 0.05 instead of ship speed from 5 to 15 knots with a step size of 5 knots.
The performance of the original Design Tool is validated by comparing the wave
added resistance transfer functions predicted by the original Design Tool with the
direct calculations from nine diﬀerent hull geometries. These diﬀer with regards
to length-to-breadth ratio, breadth-to-draught ratio, and block coeﬃcient.
Based on the performance validation the Design Tool is expanded to predict the
wave added resistance for a larger span of length-to-breadth ratio and breadth-to-
==PAGE:18==
4
Introduction
draught ratio. Furthermore, the block coeﬃcient is introduced as an additional
input parameter to the Design Tool.
The performance of the improved Design Tool validated by application of eight
diﬀerent hull geometries, and compared with model test results. The Design
Tool is implemented into an Excel workbook and a MATLAB package, and made
publicly available, as a simple program for estimating the wave added resistance
of ships based on the three main dimensions and the block coeﬃcient.
1.3
Structure of the Thesis
The content of this work is presented in a total of nine chapters composed as
follows:
Chapter 2 concerns the theoretical background of the methods used to calculate
the wave added resistance in a natural seaway.
Chapter 3 identiﬁes the limitations inherent in the original Design Tool, and out-
lines the computational methodology for developing the database containing the
wave added resistance transfer functions, as well as the interpolation procedure
performed among the transfer functions based on input parameters.
Chapter 4 concerns the validation and performance of the original Design Tool
by application of nine diﬀerent hull geometries, all of which are substantially
diﬀerent from the ﬁve reference hull geometries used to build the database.
Chapter 5 describes the modiﬁcation performed to the original Design Tool to
achieve an improved applicability.
Chapter 6 concerns the validation and performance of the modiﬁed Design Tool,
against a eight diﬀerent hull geometries diﬀering from the six reference hull
geometries used to build the database.
Chapter 7 presents the improved Design Tool implemented into an Excel work-
book and MATLAB package.
Finally, chapter 8 and 9 constitute the discussion, the conclusions and recom-
mendations for future work.
The work included in this study has focused on the performance and validation
of the Design Tool’s ability to predict the wave added resistance accurately based
==PAGE:19==
1.3Structure of the Thesis
5
on a few input parameters, being length, Lpp, breadth, B, draught, TD, and
block coeﬃcient Cb. In this matter a benchmarking of the transfer functions
predicted by the tool against results obtained by direct calculations of diﬀerent
hull geometries, using the exact same computational methodology, have been
performed. Therefore, a large number of comparative plots have been generated,
where the most signiﬁcant ones are included in the main report, while additional
results are presented in the appendices.
==PAGE:20==
6
Introduction
==PAGE:21==
Chapter 2
Mathematical Formulation
2.1
Five Degree of Freedom Linear Strip Theory
The concept of the strip theory was ﬁrst introduced by (Korvin-Kroukovsky
and Jacobs, 1957) in their paper on how strip theory is applied to predict heave
and pitch motion of a ship in regular head sea, and with a method suitable
for numerical computations. The theory was later extended by (Jacobs, 1958),
and now a days the theory has been further modiﬁed and extended, as more
sophisticated motion theories have been derived and more accurate testing
facilities for experimental data are available. Today, it is recognized, that the
original strip theory is one of the most signiﬁcant contributors to the ﬁeld
of seakeeping (Salvesen et al., 1970). Today various approaches are available
for predicting ship motions fairly accurate. Among computational seakeeping
methods are; linear ship motion strip theory, 3D Green Function Method (GFM)
and Rankine Singularity Method (RSM), and Computational Fluid Dynamics
(CFD) codes (Bertram, 2016). The linear strip theory by (Salvesen et al., 1970)
is utilized in present study, and the theory is described in a general sense in the
following.
Assume that the ship is oscillating with linear and harmonic motions with small
amplitudes. Then let (x, y, z) be a right-handed Cartesian coordinate system
ﬁxed to the mean position of the ship with z vertically upward through the
==PAGE:22==
8
Mathematical Formulation
center of gravity (COG), and x in the direction of forward ship motion. The
origin is in the x, y plane of the undisturbed free surface in accordance to ﬁgure

Figure 2.1: Visualization of the sign convention for translatory and angular
displacement of a ship freely ﬂoating in the free surface.
To estimate the wave added resistance using the radiated energy method by
(Salvesen, 1978).
It requires the ship’s response, added mass and damping
coeﬃcients, which is done by linear ship motion strip theory (Salvesen et al.,
1970). The linear ship motion strip theory consist of sectioning the ship hull
into a series of strips and thereby calculating the hydrodynamic forces on the
hull. The general 3D problem associated with estimating the ship motions are
solved by using 2D potential ﬂow theory, thus the viscous eﬀects are disregarded
so that the motion of the ﬂuid particle can be assumed irrotational.
The hydrodynamic forces acting on the hull of a ship advancing in waves is
determined by integrating the ﬂuid pressure over the wetted hull surface. By
using the Bernoulli equation for the pressure in the ﬂuid the hydrodynamic forces
and moments can be expressed by the integral equation:
Hj = −

where j = 1, 2...6 denotes the six of modes motion as deﬁned in ﬁgure 2.1, Sw
denotes the mean wetted hull surface for which the pressure is integrated over,
this yields unless otherwise stated, nj is the normal oriented positive into the
ﬂuid domain, g is the gravitational acceleration, and z is the vertical position
deﬁned in ﬁgure 2.1. The velocity potential Φ in the ﬂuid is decomposed into
two potentials, one being the time-independent steady contribution due to the
forward motion of the ship and the other the time-dependent potential associated
with the incident wave system and unsteady body motion:
Φ(x, y, z; t) = [−Ux + φs(x, y, z)] + ℜ

2.1Five Degree of Freedom Linear Strip Theory
9
where [−Ux+φs] is the steady contribution with U the forward speed of the ship
in calm water, φT is the complex amplitude of the unsteady potential, ωe is the
frequency of encounter in the moving reference frame (x′, y′, z′) i.e. the frequency
of the encountering waves experienced on the ship, and not to be confused with
the wave frequency due to forward speed and heading. This relation arises due
to the relative motion between the advancing ship and the progressing wave.
The frequency of encounter is given by (2.3), in analogy with ﬁgure 2.2.

Figure 2.2: Visualization of encounter frequency.
Each of the potentials in (2.2) must satisfy the Laplace equation and appropriate
conditions at inﬁnity, the hull boundary condition, and the linearized free-
surface condition. It is assumed that, 1) the hull geometry is such that the
steady perturbation potential and its derivatives are small, i.e. small changes
in geometry in the longitudinal direction. And 2) by only considering small
oscillating motions, so that the potential φT and its derivatives are also assumed
small.
For modern ship geometries with submerged transom one could question the
fulﬁllment of these assumptions, as signiﬁcant changes appears in the aft part
of the ship. However, for moderate to higher speeds, one could argue that the
ﬂuid particle does not experience any signiﬁcant changes along the streamline
around transom stern. By these assumptions the problem can be linearized by
disregarding higher order terms in the steady perturbation potential and in the
time-dependent potential φT , and in terms with cross products between the two
potentials.
==PAGE:24==
10
Mathematical Formulation
The time-dependent velocity potential is associated with two sub-problems
(Faltinsen, 1998), also visualized in ﬁgure 2.3:
• The forces and moments on the ship when it is restrained from oscillating
and the incident regular wave encounter the ship. The hydrodynamic loads
constitute of the wave excitation loads, and constitute of the Froude-Kriloﬀ
and the diﬀraction forces and moments. The excitation loads are obtained
as a result of variation in pressure due to the incident waves.
• The forces and moments on the ship when it is forced to oscillate with the
wave excitation frequency in any rigid-body motion mode in calm water.
In this case, no incident wave are present and the hydrodynamic loads
constitute of added mass, damping and hydrostatic re-storing terms.
Figure 2.3: Visualization of the hydrodynamic problem separated into the two
sub-problems; the excitation problem and the radiation problem (Faltinsen,
1998).

This is a critical assumption to the strip theory method, which limits theory
to short wave lengths and relatively moderate Froude numbers, and method
becomes questionable for longer wave lengths. The heave and pitch motions
are dominated by hydrostatic re-storing forces in the short wave lengths range,
which is disregarded by this assumption, though predictions for these motions in
short wave lengths shows high accuracy. Thus, theory might be used in the low
wave lengths range. For motions without re-storing force, surge, sway, and yaw,
this assumptions will have severe consequences. This concludes the formulation
of the linear conditions on the potentials.

the hydrodynamic forces associated with surge motion are signiﬁcantly smaller
than the other ﬁve modes of motions, which also applies for the response as of
the same order of magnitude. Thus it is consistent within these assumption to
exclude surge.

2.1.1
Assumption to the Linear Ship Motion Strip Theory
The assumptions imposed for the linear ship motion strip theory are also appli-
cable in the theory for estimating wave added resistance developed by (Salvesen,
1978). Recapitulating the most signiﬁcant assumptions:
• The hull is slender with only small gradual changes in the form geometry in
the longitudinal direction and has lateral symmetry. The ship is slender in
the sense, that two of its main dimensions are small compared to its length
(roughly Lpp/B > 3). The ship’s cross section do not produce longitudinal
waves.
• The forward speed of the ship should be less than Fn = 0.4.
• The frequency of encounter should not be too low or too high i.e. not too
long or too short waves.
• The viscous eﬀects are disregarded for the ﬂuid motions to be assumed
irrotational, enabling the problem to be solved based on potential theory.
Consequently, the only damping considered is the damping due to the
energy dissipation associated with generation of free surface waves. The
viscous damping eﬀects are small for the vertical ship motion, thus this
assumption can be justiﬁed.
• The linear ship motion strip theory is based on linearity. The oscillating
motions of the ship are assumed small relative to the cross sectional
dimensions of the ship, linear and harmonic.
• The steady perturbation potential Φs is assumed small, so that cross-
product between the steady perturbation potential and the time-dependent
unsteady potential ΦT can be neglected.
==PAGE:32==
18
Mathematical Formulation
• The wave-resistance perturbation potential and its derivatives are assumed
small and thereby negligible. This assumption prescribes, that the free
surface waves generated by the ship advancing at constant speed in calm
water, do not aﬀect the ship motions i.e. that the transverse and diverging
waves from the Kelvin wave pattern has no inﬂuence on the ship motions.
• The geometric hull form above the free surface level is not considered.
2.2
Wave Added Resistance
The ship advancing in a natural seaway is experiencing a total hydrodynamic
resistance, which roughly can be separated into two main contributors; calm
water resistance and added resistance.
The calm water resistance is deﬁned as the resistance experienced by a ship
advancing at a constant speed, U, in calm water conditions, which balances the
thrust, T, delivered by the propeller.
The added resistance on a ship advancing in a natural seaway resulting in
an involuntary speed loss, generally constitutes several diﬀerent components;
added resistance due to windage on the superstructure and hull, steering correc-
tions in terms of rudder correction increasing the sway force and yaw moment,
fouling of the submerged hull area, and the wave added resistance (Bertram,
2016). Focusing on the wave added resistance, hence being the most signiﬁcant
contributor.
The wave added resistance is deﬁned as the mean of the unsteady force caused
by the encountering waves, and working opposite the sailing direction in the
longitudinal direction. The total hydrodynamic resistance is presented in ﬁgure
2.4 as the sum of the calm water resistance and the mean of the unsteady
resistance.
The wave added resistance is a higher-order problem, which is
diﬃcult to predict with good accuracy, and robust and reliable methods are
generally needed on the topic. In adverse weather conditions the wave added
resistance can easily exceed the calm weather resistance, which especially applies
for slow steaming ships (Nielsen, 2015), though lower speeds does not necessarily
mean less wave added resistance.
The wave added resistance of a ship advancing in a natural seaway consisting
of long wavelengths, the horizontal distance between one wave crest and the
next, can be estimated by means of a far-ﬁeld method or a near-ﬁeld method.
Among commenly known far-ﬁeld methods are Maruo’s (1960) far-ﬁeld method,
==PAGE:33==
2.2Wave Added Resistance
19
Figure 2.4: Visualization of the resistance components of a ship advancing a
natural seaway with constant speed (Bingham, 2015).
Joosen’s (1966) and Newman’s (1967) strip method, Gerritsma & Beukelman’s
(1972) and Salvesen’s (1978) radiated energy method, and Kashiwagi et al.’s
(2009) enhanced uniﬁed theory method. For the near-ﬁeld methods are Faltinsen
et al.’s (1980) strip method and asymptotic approach in short waves, Grue &
Biberg’s (1993), Ye & Hsiung ’s(1997), Choi et al.’s (2000) wave Green function
method, and Kim & Kim’s (2011) rankine panel method in time domain and
direct simulation in irregular waves method.
Among mentioned methods, two are utilized in this study; the Salvesen’s radiated
energy method (Salvesen, 1978) for the long wavelengths, and Faltinsen’s near-
ﬁeld asymptotic approach (Faltinsen et al., 1980) for the short wavelengths. These
theoretical methods are easily implemented in a numerical program for calculating
the wave added resistance, and the methods have shown good coherence with
experimental results (Seo et al., 2013).
2.2.1
Salvesen’s Method
The far-ﬁeld method developed by (Salvesen, 1978) estimates the wave added
resistance based on the changes in the second-order momentum far away from
the ship by calculating the radiated wave energy from the ship’s six modes of
motion. The wave added resistance computed by the radiated energy method
is dominated by the heave and pitch motion, as demonstrated by (Duran and
Andersen, 2015) the inﬂuence of the number of ship motion modes used for
calculating the wave added resistance. The study showed a maximum diﬀerence
of 5% for heading close to beam sea, when using sway, heave, roll, pitch and yaw
compared to solely using heave and pitch. The gain of including the force and
moments contributing from all ﬁve modes in the Salvesen’s method is limited
to less than the accuracy of the method. Thus, we are solely considering the
heave and pitch motions as contributing to the wave added resistance in this
study. The wave added resistance is largest near the resonance heave and pitch
==PAGE:34==
20
Mathematical Formulation
response, thus the critical factor is the relation between the peak response and
the peak wave energy.
Salvesen directs his concerns towards the consequence of violating the assumption,
that the potential φB (2.51) due to body disturbance, including body diﬀraction,
should be signiﬁcantly less that the potential Φ0 (2.54) of the incident wave i.e.
φB ≪φ0. This assumption is expected to lead to less accurate results for surface
ships at zero forward speed and in beam and near-beam to following waves. The
body potential is:
φB =
X
j=2,3
ξjφj + φ7
(2.51)
where φj=2,3 is the contribution to the velocity potential from the heave and
pitch motion of the ship and φ7 is the diﬀraction potential.

by:

2.2.2
Faltinsen’s Asymptotic Method
In short wavelengths, it is diﬃcult to calculate the wave added resistance with
good accuracy by means of theStrip theory method, as the hydrodynamic eﬀects
are dominated by the bow diﬀraction wave. The radiated wave induced added
resistance is small for short wavelengths, and assumed negligible in Faltinsen’s
asymptotic method.
The Faltinsen’s asymptotic method is a direct pressure integration method also
referred to as a near-ﬁeld method. The wave added resistance is estimated by
integrating the pressure along the wetted hull surface encountered by the incident
wave. The ship’s hull side encountered by the incident wave is assumed to be
a vertical wall at the waterplane and the wave height is small compared to the
ship’s draught. Furthermore, is the incident wave assumed to be fully reﬂected
by the ship side i.e. no wave amplitude on the shadow side. The assumption of
the short wave length provides that the wave excitation forces are negligible, and
it is only the part of the ship close to the waterplane that will aﬀect the ﬂow
ﬁeld. This implies that resonance oscillations are highly unlike to be present,
and therefore the inﬂuence of the wave induced motions of the ship are absent
and negligible. The method is based on potential ﬂow theory, hence all viscous
eﬀects are neglected. Furthermore, the diﬀraction eﬀects from sharp corners and
rapidly changing geometry of the ship in the waterplane are small over a wave
length.
The mean drift force components and yaw moments is given by (Faltinsen et al.,
1980):
Rw(ω) =
Z
L
¯Fi nidl
(2.67)

2.3
Wave Added Resistance in Irregular Seas
In order to predict the wave added resistance of a ship advancing in a natural
seaway, constituting of irregular waves, it is necessary to assume, that the
principle of superposition applies to the added resistance problem, and that the
irregular sea can be represented by an energy spectrum S(ω), then the mean
wave added resistance in a seaway, can for a ship advancing with constant speed
and heading, be expressed by (Faltinsen, 1998):
¯Rwj = 2
Z ∞
0

ζ2w

for j = 1, 2, ..., 6
(2.69)
where ¯Rw(ω)/ζ2
w is the non-dimensionless wave added resistance due to the
squared of the incident wave amplitude from (2.52) or (2.67). S(ω) is the wave
energy spectrum.
2.4
Wave Energy Spectrum
To determine the wave added resistance on a ship advancing at constant speed
and heading in a natural seaway one needs to describe the energy of the irregular
waves. The natural seaway constitute of irregular waves, which are representable
as a superposition of an inﬁnite number of small amplitude regular waves
having diﬀerent frequencies, directions of propagation, amplitudes, and phases.
Each frequency and direction describes a wave components with its associated
amplitude and phase.
This allows the free surface wave proﬁle to be described by the inﬁnite sum
==PAGE:39==
2.4Wave Energy Spectrum
25
of wave components, illustrated in ﬁgure 2.7. The wave energy spectrum, also
called wave amplitude energy density spectrum, describes the energy per square
meter of the sea surface of the n’th wave component. It is deﬁned so that the
area bounded by a frequency range is proportional to the total energy of all the
wave components within the range of the frequencies.
Figure 2.7: Representation of the waves in a long-crested short term sea state
showing the superposition principle in the time domain and the corresponding
wave spectrum in the frequency domain (Faltinsen et al., 1980).
To describe the complexity of natural sea surface, the concept of a wave spectrum
is commonly used. The wave energy spectrum models are applied to obtain
a reasonable estimate of the entire wave energy spectrum, S(ω), from known
values like the signiﬁcant wave height, Hs, and wave period, T. These known
parameters may be obtained by direct measurements from equipment onboard
the ship, hindcasts or visual observation, the last contributing with the largest
uncertainties.
For the purpose of present study, two wave energy spectra are utilized; the
Bretschneider wave spectrum, which represents the fully developed sea at the
open ocean, and the JONSWAP (Joint North Sea Wave Project) wave energy
spectrum, which is a modiﬁcation to the Bretschneider wave energy spectrum
representing the North Sea and coastal areas. A convenient expansion to the
Design Tool would be to introduce a wave spectrum model in depth-limited
==PAGE:40==
26
Mathematical Formulation
waters e.g. TMS (Texel-Marsen-Arsloe) spectrum, being a modiﬁed version of
the JONSWAP.
2.4.1
Bretschneider Wave Energy Spectrum
The Bretschneider wave energy spectrum is adapted by the International Towing
Tank Conference (ITTC) as a standard wave energy spectrum to represent the
conditions occurring at open ocean. The Bretschneider wave energy spectrum is
expressed by (Nielsen, 2010):
SB(ω) = As
ω5 exp

(2.70)
where

where Hs is the signiﬁcant wave height, and Tz is the mean zero up-crossing
period. It applies for the Bretschneider spectrum, that the relationship is valid
(Jensen, 2001):
T0 = 1.296 T = 1.41 Tz
(2.73)
where T is the wave period, and T0 is the modal period.
2.4.2
JONSWAP Wave Energy Spectrum
The JONSWAP wave energy spectrum is used to describe ocean waves in a
growing phase. It describes the wave spectra growing in fetch limited conditions,
meaning, that the wave growth appears under a steady oﬀshore wind limited by
the shorter distance to the shore line. The shape of the spectrum is similar to
the Bretschneider spectrum, though the spectral peak is distributed on a more
narrow frequency range. The JONSWAP wave energy spectrum is expressed by
(Nielsen, 2010):
SJ(ω) = 0.658 C SB(ω)
(2.74)
where
C = 3.3J

2.5
Calculated Ship Parameters
The majority of the parameters required to compute the added mass, damping
coeﬃcient, ship response, wave added resistance in short and long wave lengths,
and wave added resistance in irregular waves are obtained in I-ship. Ship speciﬁc
parameters not available or computed by the utilized software is described in
the following.
The vertical center of gravity (VCG) is approximated based in the design draught,
TD, for each ship, and measured vertically upwards perpendicular to the even
keel. See ﬁgure 2.1 and note that d denotes TD:
V CG = 0.95 TD
(2.78)
The pitch radius of gyration is approximated by (Conference, 2002):
r55 = 0.25 Lpp
(2.79)
The roll and yaw radius of gyration are approximated based on the ship type.
The ship geometries with high block coeﬃcient, namely tanker and bulk carriers,
are approximated based in the ships beam, B, by (Conference, 2002):
r44 = r66 = 0.35 B
(2.80)
and for container ship and ferries:
r44 = r66 = 0.40 B
(2.81)

Throughout the present study the wave added resistance and the wave frequency
are converted to non-dimensional quantities.

==PAGE:43==
Chapter 3
The Design Tool Model
Present chapter describes the principal steps performed in re-constructing the
original Design Tool, using the methodology developed by (Nielsen, 2015), though
the speed (knots) are substituted with Froude number and increased in resolution.
The database, of the re-constructed Design Tool, holds wave added resistance
transfer functions for 10 wave headings from beam to head seas (β = 90◦−180◦),
and each heading holds 6 ship speeds from Fn = 0.00 to Fn = 0.25. Wave
headings from following to beam seas (β = 0◦−90◦) are taken to contribute with
zero wave added resistance to the level of accuracy we are striving to achieve
within this study. Furthermore, the accuracy of the applied method (Salvesen,
1978) is not trusted in following and quartering seas. Though a comprehensive
experimental study (Valanto and Hong, 2015) showed, that in following to beam
seas the wave added resistance values are generally less than in bow quartering
(β ≈145◦) with the highest values, but not of insigniﬁcant magnitude and should
not be overlooked. Despite knowledge about following to beam seas’ eﬀect on
the wave added resistance these wave headings are excluded from present study.
==PAGE:44==
30
The Design Tool Model
3.1
Ship Statistics Data
In order to develop a useful design tool enabling rule makers and naval architects
to quickly assess the wave added resistance based on the ship’s initial design
particulars of length L, breadth B, and design draught TD, a set four parameters
are deﬁned. The four parameters are the length-breadth ratio (Lpp/B), the
breadth-draught ratio (B/TD), ship speed (Fn), and the wave heading relative
to the ship’s bow (β), which covers the majority of tankers and bulk carriers in
the world ﬂeet. Based on the data shown in ﬁgure 3.1 the majority of tankers
and bulk carriers lies within the ranges 5 ≥B/Lpp ≥7 and 2 ≥B/TD ≥3.2.
These four values, yields the extremities bracketing the Design Tool database.
Five reference hull geometries, representing the vast majority of bulk carriers
and tankers in the world ﬂeet, were scaled to each of the four scaling ratios. The
four combinations of scaling ratios appears in table 3.1 and the ﬁve reference
geometries forming the basis for the database are shown in Appendicies A.1, A.2,
A.3, A.4, and A.5.
Figure 3.1: Lpp/B-ratio and B/TD-ratio of tanker and bulk carriers in the period
1990 - 2013 (Nielsen, 2015). Data collection from Signiﬁcant Ships.
Table 3.1: Four combinations of scaling ratios chosen for the initial development
of the Design Tool (Nielsen, 2015).

3.2Computational Methodology
31
3.2
Computational Methodology
By the determination of the four scaling ratios the wave added resistance trans-
fer functions are computed by means of the DTU developed software I-ship,
Salvesen’s Strip theory method, and Faltinsen’s Asymptotic method. The com-
putational methodology is described in the following.
3.2.1
Applied Software - I-ship
The ﬁve reference geometries are scaled linearly to the four combinations of
scaling ratios keeping the length, Lpp, constant, yielding 20 hull geometries.
Each hull geometry is deﬁned in I-ship featuring tools for ship design, such as
hull design, resistance prediction, seakeeping, intact and damage stability etc.
Utilizing the software, hydrostatic analysis are performed on the 20 hull ge-
ometries in order to determine the longitudinal center of gravity, LCG, and
displacement, ∇, at design draught with zero trim. It should be noted, that a
variation in the draught at aft perpendicular, AP, and fore perpendicular, FP, of
±0.015 m is accepted. The weight deﬁnition is modiﬁed, in a manual iterative
manner, until the desired loading condition is achieved. The wave heading vector
and forward speed vector is deﬁned in the introduction to this chapter. The
method by (Salvesen et al., 1970) for estimating ship motions and the Green
function for hydrodynamic coeﬃcients are applied using the strip theory method,
and the motion results are given as frequency response functions over an angular
wave frequency range 0.15 ≤ω ≤6rad/s.
Based on the provided input a linear ﬁve degree of freedom strip theory analysis
(Salvesen et al., 1970) is performed. The relevant ship speciﬁc parameters used
in the software appears in appendix A. I-ship provides the response amplitude
operator (RAO) functions and hydrodynamic coeﬃcients. The RAO functions
express the proportion of the wave amplitude or wave slope amplitude transferred
by the system (ship) into ship motions. The general workﬂow performed in
I-ship is visualized in ﬁgure 3.2.
3.2.2
Wave Added Resistance Transfer Functions
The wave added resistance transfer functions are computed for the 20 hull
geometries at every heading and speed using a number of evenly spaced non-
dimensional wave frequencies, in the range 0.6 ≤¯ω ≤10, and the two methods
==PAGE:46==
32
The Design Tool Model
Figure 3.2: Workﬂow for each operation performed in I-ship to obtain RAO
functions and hydrodynamic coeﬃcients. The blue boxes symbolize the required
input levels, grey boxes symbolize path levels in the software, red boxes symbolize
fulﬁllment statements, and green boxes symbolize the desired output from the
software.
described in section 2.2. For each condition of hull geometry, heading and speed
the intersection point in the high frequency range between the curve calculated
by the Strip theory and the Asymptotic method are determined, and the two
curves are joined to produce one result. The procedure is shown in ﬁgure 3.3a.
For frequencies less then the intersection point the values computed by the
Strip theory are used, and at higher frequencies the values computed by the
Asymptotic method are used.
Occasionally, no intersection point is identiﬁed between the two methods, see
ﬁgure 3.3b and 3.3c. If the Asymptotic method is resulting in larger values, than
the Strip theory method, then a ﬁctive intersection point is created between
the two curves at the frequency corresponding to the peak value of the Strip
theory curve. This is resulting in a "jump" in the joined transfer function at the
discrete frequency, which may contribute to a decreased accuracy. It should be
noted, that this behavior is only seen in a few cases, and the magnitude of the

3.3
Design Tool Interpolation
Having the 240 transfer functions for wave added resistance to constitute the
Design Tool database, then a sequence of linear interpolations are performed
based on inputs to the Design Tool to predict the ﬁnal wave added resistance for
any given input parameters. Is the parameter input values outside the scaling
ratios bracketing the database, then linear extrapolation is performed.
The initial interpolation is done for the wave heading generating two transfer
functions for the speed-interpolation resulting in one transfer function valid for
the given input speed and wave heading. The interpolation process is repeated
for every combination of scaling ratio, yielding four transfer functions. Figure 3.5
shows a visualization of the interpolation for heading and speed. Subsequently,
the ﬁnal two linear interpolation are performed according to the given (Lpp/B)-
ratio and (B/TD)-ratio generating the ﬁnal transfer function for the wave added
resistance. The interpolation is ﬁrst performed with respect to the (B/TD)-ratio,
and then the (Lpp/B)-ratio, which is shown in ﬁgure 3.6.
All linear interpolation are performed using:
y = y0 + (y0 −y1) x −x0
x1 −x0
(3.1)

==PAGE:51==
Chapter 4
Validation and Performance
of the Original Design Tool
Present chapter concerns the evaluation and validation of the Design Tool
developed based on hull geometries with a high block coeﬃcient. Emphasis is
laid upon the Design Tool’s ability to estimated the transfer function for the
wave added resistance in cases where linear extrapolation of the B/TD-ratio and
the Lpp/B-ratio occurs.
The linear interpolation procedure is validated by comparing the Design Tool
estimated transfer functions and the direct calculated transfer functions of the
un-scaled "original" hull geometries, which the Design Tool database is based on.
The direct calculations are performed in accordance to the Strip theory method,
Asymptotic method, and merging approach.
Eight diﬀerent hull geometries, with respect to main dimensions and block
coeﬃcient, have been used to validate the applicability of the original Design
Tool.
==PAGE:52==
38
Validation and Performance of the Original Design Tool
4.1
Validation of Linear Interpolation
Generally, a good coherence between the Design Tool and the direct calculation
are observed. Though, the Design Tool has a tendency to under-estimate the peak
value in the low frequency range, where Strip theory is applied. The maximum
discrepancy of the peak value in the low frequency range is approximately 25%
for the VLCC.

Figure 4.1: Validation of the linear interpolation process in the Design Tool. The
hull geometry is the Tanker.
Figure 4.1 shows a tendency of the Strip theory, which is observed to be consistent
for all analyzed hull geometries. The values predicted at Fn = 0.00 has a larger
peak amplitude, then values predicted for Fn = 0.05 i.e.
the wave added
resistance decreases for a ship at zero speed and initiating to a forward speed
of Fn = 0.05, and increased again as picking up speed Fn > 0.05. Being an
un-physical behavior, this also suggest an over-estimation of the wave added
resistance at zero forward speed when utilizing the Strip theory. The observed
tendency corresponds well with the weakness of the Strip theory stated in
(Salvesen, 1974), that the results are expected to be less accurate at zero forward
speed and in beam and near-beam waves. Moreover, (Salvesen, 1978), states that
stern-quartering and following wave headings also would generate less accurate
==PAGE:53==
4.1Validation of Linear Interpolation
39
results. It should be mentioned, that this do not aﬀect the development of the
Design Tool, as following wave headings are excluded in this study.
The deviation in the high frequency range is prone to increase in near-beam
to quartering seas β = 110◦to β = 150◦, and with ampliﬁcation for increased
forward speeds. This is a general tendency, though the most signiﬁcant behavior
is seen for the Seahorse 35.
Figure 4.2 shows this behavior with increased
deviation until a certain bow quartering wave heading is achieved, hereafter the
two high frequency curves align well. It should be noted, that the severity of
the deviation between the two high frequency curves are nothing alike for the
remaining four hull geometries. See Appendix C further results.

==PAGE:54==
40
Validation and Performance of the Original Design Tool
4.2
Performance Validation
The performance of the Design Tool is validated by comparing the transfer
functions for wave added resistance with the direction calculations of nine
diﬀerent hull geometries, with respect to length, breadth, draught, and block
coeﬃcient.
The objective is to test the applicability of the Design Tool, by introducing
input values, which are violating the Lpp/B-ratios and B/TD-ratios bracketing
the database. And block coeﬃcients which diﬀers signiﬁcantly from the range
spanned by the ﬁve reference geometries.
The analysis includes a series of slender and a few blunt hull geometries, where
each hull geometry and main particulars are presented in the associated section.
Analysis of ﬁve hull geometries are presented in this section with the purpose of
constraining the triviality of the section. These hull geometries are representing
the the main ﬁndings comprehensively. Analysis of the four remaining geometries
are presented in appendix D.
4.2.1
Analysis of Duisburg Test Case
The Duisburg Test Case (DTC) hull is an imitation of a modern container ship,
which is widely use in academical studies, thus model test results and CFD
results are easily accessible. The DTC is a design of a typical 14.000 TEU
container ship with a single-screw, bulbous bow, large ﬂare, large stern overhang,
and a wetted transom. The main particulars of the DTC hull appears in table
4.1. The geometry of the DTC is described by 25 station curves with increased
density in the aft and fore part. The geometry is shown in ﬁgure 4.3.
Figure 4.3: Geometry representation of the Duisburg Test Case 14.000 TEU
container ship.

4.2.1.1
Wave Added Resistance
The DTC hull geometry lays outside the upper B/TD-ratio value bracketing
the Design Tool database, hence forcing the tool to extrapolate to estimate the
transfer function for wave added resistance, though the extrapolation is limited
in magnitude. Moreover, the Cb = 0.66 is somewhat lower than the average
Cb = 0.82 for the ﬁve reference geometries.
For ship speeds less than Fn = 0.10 a good agreement is generally seen for
frequencies less than ¯ω = 3, equal to a normalized wavelength of λ/Lpp ≈0.7 and
longer waves. Hereafter increased deviation evolves for headings from near-beam
to bow quartering waves. From bow quartering to head waves, only small changes
in discrepancy between the two curves are seen, thus being relatively constant.
Generally, a shift in the phase of the peak value is observed, where the Design
Tool generally appears at a lower frequency.
For ship speeds higher than Fn = 0.10 the phase of the peak aligns relatively
good, however the Design Tool over-estimates the peak value at up to 30%. A
weak coherence in the high frequency range, where the Asymptotic method is
applied, is observed, though near-beam waves coincide well.

between the curves is seen for wave heading β = 150◦. Comparison between the

smaller reefer container ships with a high operational speed. The main particulars
of the Reefer appears in table 4.2. The geometry is described in ﬁgure 4.5 with
30 station curves and increased density in the fore and aft part of the hull.

Tool under-estimates in this range. Contrary, the Design Tool over-estimates

over-estimation in the high frequency range increases in magnitude for increased

surface level, thus the over-estimation for bow quartering to head waves are
==PAGE:58==
44
Validation and Performance of the Original Design Tool
expected of the original Design Tool. The reference geometries are blunt in the
bow region, reﬂecting a large amount of ﬂuid, which provides a large area for the
ﬂuid pressure to impact yielding a large resistance force. Whereas, the Reefer
has a slender and wave piercing waterplane area in the bow region, which reﬂects
a smaller amount of water yielding a lower resistance force. Figure 2.6 illustrates
the change in wave added resistance in short wavelengths as a function of the
geometric shape of the waterplane area in the bow region.
Figure 4.6 shows a series of transfer functions, and the described tendencies are
well illustrated. Comparison between the two curves for a number of headings
and ship speeds appears in Appendix E.

evolving under-estimation with increased ship speeds and wave headings going
towards head seas β = 180◦. The Design Tool is not able to predict the wave
added resistance in short wavelength (higher frequencies) with good accuracy
for the KVLCC2, due to the more full bow region compared to the reference
geometries.

4.2.5
Analysis of 109m RoPax
The 109m RoPax is a twin-screw roll-on roll-oﬀ1.500 passenger capacity car ferry

bow. The main particulars appears in table 4.5. The geometry is described in

4.2.5.1
Wave Added Resistance
The 109m RoPax hull geometry lays signiﬁcantly outside the upper B/TD-ratio
value and inside the lower Lpp/B-ratio bracketing the database, which yields
the Design Tool to linearly extrapolate the obtain results. The Cb = 0.65 is
somewhat less than the range spanned by the reference geometries. The hull
geometry is diverging signiﬁcantly from the reference geometries, hence being a
twin-screw with a skeg, and having an extended U-shape in the aft part.
The Design Tool over-estimates the peak values with up 100% for head waves
and ship speeds above Fn = 0.15. Generally, the phase of the peak is shifted, so
that the Design Tool peaks at a lower frequency. A reasonable coherence is seen
in the high frequency range for speeds Fn ≥0.10 and headings from β = 110◦
to β = 140◦. Hereafter, over-estimations evolves with waves headings gooing
towards head seas.
The 109m RoPax geometry demonstrates that the Design Tool is constrained in
its ability to estimate the transfer functions, when extrapolating the B/TD-ratio.
Moreover, the diverging hull geometry, i.e. extreme U-shape in the aft part,
twin-screw, and skeg, is presumed to have some inﬂuence, which is not accounted
for by the Design Tool.
Figure 4.12 shows a series of transfer function comparisons, which shows a grasp
of the performance of the Design Tool. Additional results appears in Appendix
E.

4.3
Summary of Validation and Performance
Generelly, the Design Tool predicts the wave added resistance with reasonable
accuracy for hull geometries, which are within the Lpp/B-ratio and B/TD-ratio
values bracketing the database, even though, that the block coeﬃcient of the
tested hull geometries are somewhat less than the range spanned by the ﬁve
reference geometries. The comparison showed a good consistency in the wave
added resistance transfer functions predicted by the Strip theory as a function of
wave heading. However, inconsistency in the results computed by the Asymptotic
method was observed.
A phase shift and under-estimation of the peak value in the low frequency range is
generally seen. Moreover, the Design Tool performs purely in the high frequency
range for geometries with a slender and wave piercing shape of the waterplane
area in the bow region. This tendency, is in agreement with the sensitivity of
the Asymptotic method for head waves, and the fact that the original Design
Tool is based on hull geometries with a full bow region.
==PAGE:66==
52
Validation and Performance of the Original Design Tool
The maximum observed discrepancy in the peak values lays in the range between
25% - 30%. The accuracy of wave added resistance calculated based on linear
ship motion strip theory compared to experimental results cannot be expected
to better than 20% - 30%, assuming that the ship motions are predeicted
with an accuracy of 15% (Salvesen, 1978, page 25). This level of accuracy is
acceptable present study. Considering the results from the validation of the linear
interpolation in Appendix C, it is seen, that the Design Tool under-estimates
the peak value for the reference geometries. This indicates, that the size of the
interpolation span smoothens the peak value, and the Design Tool is unable to
catch the extreme peak values.
The Design Tool have shown to handle small linear extrapolation with regards
to the B/TD-ratio for higher ship speeds with an acceptable accuracy. Larger
extrapolation on one or both ratios showed aggravated accuracy in the low
frequency range, and even oscillatory behavior in some cases.
Despite the inaccuracy in the low frequency range, a more reasonable coherence
where generally seen in the higher frequency range. The discrepancies between
the curves in the high frequency range is believed to be caused by the uniformity
of the ﬁve reference geometries with regards to the shape of waterplane area in
the bow region. As earlier emphasized, the Asymptotic method is sensitive to
the bow form.
It applies for all the anaylized hull geometries, that the wave added resistance
calculated based on the strip theory results in un-physically high values. This
inaccuracy was expected from this method, as discussed in section 2.2.1.
==PAGE:67==
Chapter 5
Improving the Design Tool
Model
Present chapter concerns the modiﬁcation conducted to the original Design
Tool model with the objective to achieve improved robustness with respect to
estimation of added wave resistance for a larger diversity of hull geometries and
with reasonable accuracy.
The computational methodology described in section 3 is kept unchanged, how-
ever the main assumptions and limitations are revised, based on drawn con-
clusions from chapter 4. The implemented improvements constitute; WAMIT
computations for zero forward speed, revised Lpp/B-ratios and B/TD-ratios, an
additional input parameters in terms of the block coeﬃcient (Cb), and changes
to the linear interpolation process in the Design Tool.
5.1
Ship Statistical Data
The validation of the performance of the Design Tool concluded, that the ability
to predict the wave added resistance of a hull geometry, that violates Lpp/B-
ratio or B/TD-ratio, results in large discrepancies. Moreover, the results for the
high frequency range showed, that the Design Tool over-estimated the added
==PAGE:68==
54
Improving the Design Tool Model
resistance signiﬁcantly for slender hull geometries encountering bow quartering
to head waves.
These two main ﬁndings advocate, that the Lpp/B-ratio and B/TD-ratio brack-
eting the database are extended to cover a larger span, enabling the linear
interpolation process to span over a larger diversity of hull geometries e.g. con-
tainer ships and RoRo ferriers. Again, taking the basis in world ﬂeet statistics
of container ships and RoRo ferries the revised Lpp/B-ratio and B/TD-ratio
are deﬁned. Figure 5.1a shows the distribution of Lpp/B-ratio and B/TD-ratio
for container ships as a function of Lpp. The distribution of ships is relative
consistent with a limited degree of scatter. Figure 5.1b and 5.1c shows the a grasp
of the world distribution of RoRo passenger (RoPax) ferries and RoRo cargo
(RoRo) ferries, which reveals a large span in the Lpp/B-ratio and B/TD-ratio.
With the desire to cover a wide diversity of Lpp/B-ratios and B/TD-ratios, three
new values for the two ratios have been selected based on presented ship data.
This yields nine new combinations of scaling ratios, which appears in table 5.1.
Two additional ratios is selected to limit the size of the linear interpolation, as
the validation of the Design Tool indicated a tendency to smoothen out the peak
values. Introducing an extra point on the two interpolation lines is believed to
oppose the smoothening eﬀect, and enable the Design Tool to catch the extreme
values.

5.2
The Block Coeﬃcient as an Additional Input
Parameter
To introduce the block coeﬃcient as an additional input parameter it is decided
to divide the Design Tool database into two separate databases. One database
developed based on reference geometries with a low block coeﬃcient, Cb ≈0.6,
and a second database developed based on reference geometries with a high
block coeﬃcient, Cb ≈0.8. Three reference geometries are selected for each of
the two databases, and the selection is done with focus on creating a database
representing the range of Lpp/B-ratio and B/TD-ratio in an optimal way i.e.
a geometry from each corner of the square stretched by the two ratios and Cb
values. The six reference geometries constituting the two databases are shown
in table 5.2. Introducing nine scaling combination and six reference geometries
==PAGE:70==
56
Improving the Design Tool Model
yields 54 geometries to compute the added wave resistance on, which more
than doubles the computation eﬀort associated with developing the databases,
compared to the original Design Tool.

5.3
Zero Forward Speed - WAMIT
During the re-construction of the original Design Tool database, it was observed,
that the Strip theory was over-estimating the wave added resistance for zero
forward speed. The software WAMIT is utilized to improve the accuracy of the
Design Tool at zero forward speed. WAMIT is based on radiation and diﬀraction
three dimensional panel method, which is applicable for linear analysis of the
interaction of surface waves with ﬂoating, submerged, and ﬁxed solid oﬀshore
structures at zero forward speed. Focus has been on applying the software, and
the theoretical background of the software is not further described. For further
software description see (WAMIT, 2016).
WAMIT calculations have been performed on two hull geometries for each of the
nine scaling combinations (S1, S2,..., S9). The two hull geometries was selected
to represent the group of low block coeﬃcient geometries and the group of
high block coeﬃcient, thus the most average geometry was identiﬁed based on
Lpp/B-ratio, B/TD-ratio, and block coeﬃcient, the last weighing heaviest. The
low block coeﬃcient group is represented by the 71m RoPax ferry, see appendix
D.4, and the high block coeﬃcient group by the Seahorse 35 appendix A.3.
Figure 5.2 and 5.3 shows a comparison between the wave added resistance
computed by WAMIT and the Strip theory joined with the Asymptotic method for
nine wave headings from β = 100◦to β = 180◦for the KVLCC2 and the HTC
geometry, see section 4.2.4 and appendix D.1 for ship particulars, respectively.
A fairly good coherence is seen between the two methods for near-beam to beam
waves, 100◦< β < 120◦, for the hole frequency range, however for headings from
near bow quartering to head waves, 130◦< β < 180◦, an increased discrepancy
evolves in the lower frequency range, as the wave heading approaches head sea.
==PAGE:71==
5.3Zero Forward Speed - WAMIT
57
The KVLCC2 geometry shows good coherence in the higher frequency range at
all wave headings, though the HTC geometry shows increase in discrepancy for
wave headings approaching head sea. It is clear, that the Strip theory fails to
predict the wave addd resistance at lower frequencies with reasonable accuracy
for zero forward speed, why WAMIT computations are introduced to the Design
Tool.

==PAGE:73==
5.4Design Tool Interpolation
59
5.4
Design Tool Interpolation
The linear interpolation method in the Design Tool is changed, due to the
additional input parameter, Cb, and the additional points limiting the span of
the interpolation.
The linear interpolation with regards to Lpp/B-ratio and B/TD-ratio is performed
separately for each of the two databases, providing two transfer functions for
the wave added resistance, one for a high Cb and one for a low Cb. To obtain
the ﬁnal wave added resistance transfer function an interpolation is performed
between the high and low Cb database based on the input Cbinput.
The schematic approach of the linear interpolation for the Lpp/B-ratio and B/TD-
ratio is visualized in ﬁgure 5.4. The sequence of interpolations in each of the two
databases follows the approach described in section 3.3 and shown in ﬁgure 3.5
and 3.6. However, the four combinations of scaling ratios (S1, S2, ..., S9) used
for the interpolations varies depending input of Lpp/B-ratio and B/TD-ratio.

The ﬁnal linear interpolation between the high and the low Cb database is only
performed if 0.64 < Cbinput < 0.81. If the Cbinput ≥0.81, than the high Cb
database results is used, and if Cb ≤0.61 then the low Cb database result is

used.
5.5
Salvesen’s Radiated Engergy Method
During the averaging process of the nine scaled reference geometries at each
speed and wave heading, it was observed, that the computed transfer functions
for the wave added resistance is resulting in un-physical results for geometries
with a Lpp/TD-ratio exceeding 20. The behavior of the transfer functions is
distinct at low forward ship speeds Fn < 0.1, though highly inﬂuenced by the
magnitude of the Lpp/TD-ratio. Figure 5.5 shows the most extreme case of this
behavior.
Moreover, it is evident, that the low Cb geometries are more prone to result in
these extreme transfer functions, than high Cb geometries. Additional results
illustrating this behavior is provided in appendix F.

Figure 5.5: Un-physical results of the wave added resistance for the reference

A study of the response amplitude operator (RAO) function for heave and pitch
has shown a convergence of the motions computed in I-ship, thus the source to
==PAGE:75==
5.5Salvesen’s Radiated Engergy Method
61
the un-physical transfer functions are believed to be hidden in the calculation
of the wave added resistance by the radiated energy method (Salvesen, 1978).
The results strongly suggest, that the geometries with a high Lpp/TD-ratio is
violating the limitations of the method.
Weak indications of this tendency are also seen when examining the results of the
averaging process for the original database based on the ﬁve reference geometries
with high Cb. As mentioned, the behavior is less distinct for geometries with
a high Cb, thus hard to identify for the geometries at scaling ratio S4 in the
original database.
A methodical study of the described behavior of the transfer functions aiming to
identify the source of the problem lays outside the scope of present study, thus
no further investigation into the problem are made. Despited the knowledge of
these adverse behavior evolving for higher Lpp/TD-ratio, the computed averaged
transfer functions are used in the development of the improved Design Tool
databases.
The Design Tool user should be conscious about physical credibility of the
wave added resistance results predicted by the Design Tool, when analyzing hull
geometries with high Lpp/TD-ratio at low forward ship speeds.

Improving the Design Tool Model
==PAGE:77==
Chapter 6
Validation and Performance
of the Improved Design
Tool
Present chapter concerns the validation and performance of the improved Design
Tool constituting of three step. Firstly, a validation of the linear interpolation is
performed and secondly the applicability is tested. These two steps are performed
similar to those in section 4.1 and 4.2. Thirdly, the Design Tool is validated
towards model test results for the DTC, KVLCC2 and SSPA 2020 hull. Details
on the ULYSSES 2020 concept tanker hull.
The objective of present chapter is to conﬁrm an increased robustness of the
Design Tool with regards to predicting wave added resistance with reasonable
accuracy of geometries representing a larger span of Cb, Lpp/B-ratio, and B/T-
ratio. Moreover, to evaluate the physical credibility of the improved Design
Tool.
The analysis included in the performance validation are a series of low Cb and
high Cb hull geometries. The main particulars of the geometries are described in
section 4.2, and newly geometries are introduced in the associated section.
==PAGE:78==
64
Validation and Performance of the Improved Design Tool
6.1
Validation of Linear Interpolation
Generally, a good coherence between the Design Tool and the direct calculation
is observed at all headings and forward speeds. A slight over-estimation of the
peak value at lower frequencies is seen for bow quartering to head waves and at
forward speeds exceeding Fn = 0.15. The maximum discrepancy at the peak
values is ≈30%, which is a reasonable deviation, and similar to the accuracy of
the original Design Tool database.

6.2
Performance Validation
A total of eight hull geometries have been used in the validation. The analyzed
particulars of hull geometries are described in section 4.2.
Unfortunately, only a few hull geometries with a block coeﬃcient (0.64 < Cb <

the Cbinptu are available. Hull geometries in this range are the LPG/C, DTC
container hull, and the KVLCC2 tanker hull.

for the remaining hull geometries appears in Appendix H.

6.2.1
Analysis of LPG Carrier
The LPG/C is particular in the sense that Cb = 0.71, which requires the Design
Tool to perform linear interpolation based on Cb. Figure 6.3 shows the Design
Tool prediction compared with the direct calculation of the wave added resistance
for a series of wave headings and ship speeds.
The Design Tool is still under-estimating the amplitude of the peak value in the
low frequency range, but the peak frequency aligns well for the LPG/C. The
maximum deviation around the peak amplitude is observed to 48% for head
waves at U = 0.05. Improved prediction in the high frequency range is observed.
In general, a slight improvement of the Design Tool is achieved for the LPG/C.

The under-estimation of the peak amplitude is believed to be lying in the
approach of utilizing linear interpolation in the three dimensions of Lpp/B-ratio,
B/TD-ratio, and Cb. Considering ﬁgure 6.4 showing the sequence of linear
interpolation performed in the Design Tool. Each of the upper plots presents
the ﬁrst two interpolation performed in the B/TD-direction (ﬁrst and second)
for the low Cb and high Cb database, as illustrated on ﬁgure 5.4. The lower plot
shows the four transfer functions obtained from the B/TD interpolations, the
==PAGE:81==
6.2Performance Validation
67
interpolation in the Lpp/B (third) direction, the ﬁnal Cb interpolation, and the
direct calculated transfer function.
Placing the LPG/C in the interpolation scheme in ﬁgure 5.4, it is located
in the corner close to S4, spanned by the four scaling ratios S1, S2, S4, and
S5. This suggest that the two transfer functions obtained from the B/T-ratio
interpolations should mimic the transfer functions S1 and S4 the most, which
is not the case for the peak amplitude, but the peak frequency is well aligned.
This behavior, of smoothening the peak amplitude, of the interpolated result is
iterated in all four interpolation sequences, yielding a gradually reduction of the
peak value, shown in ﬁgure 6.4. The phase shift between the transfer functions
are obviously resulting in an under-estimation of the peak value, and the larger
the phase shift, the more distinct is the smoothening of the peak amplitude.

To cope with the phase shift of the transfer functions, it is believed, that a higher
resolution of the Lpp/B-ratio, B/TD-ratio, and Cb will have a positive eﬀect on
the smoothening eﬀect of the peak amplitude. Having a span of the Lpp/B ratio,
B/TD-ratio, and Cb of 1.5, 2.0, and 0.17, respectively, is obviously resulting
in large phase shifts. Having discretized the three interpolation dimensions to
==PAGE:82==

a higher number of scaling combinations (> 9), may reduce the span of the
phase shift and provide improved estimates of the peak amplitude. However, the
consequence of a reﬁned resolution of the three dimensions of linear interpolation
would result in a signiﬁcant increase in computational eﬀort associated with
developing the databases.

6.2.3
Analysis of KVLCC2
The KVLCC2 is particular in the sense, that the Design Tool requires to perform
a small interpolation with respect the Cb = 0.80. Moreover, the geometric form
of the waterplane in the bow region is somewhat more box-shaped, than the
reference geometries. The waterplane area is shown in ﬁgure 6.6.
Figure 6.6: Waterplane area for the KVLCC2 geometry. Note the signiﬁcantly
blunt geometric shape of the waterplane area in the bow region. The non-shadow
region in head waves (β = 180◦) is colored magenta.
Reasonable coherence is seen in the low frequency range, though the peak
amplitudes are generally under-estimated.
The maximum under-estimation
around the peak amplitude is 35% in bow quartering waves, β = 140◦at
Fn = 0.25. In the higher frequency range an increased discrepancy is evolving
for wave headings from β = 160◦to β = 180◦. This is expected, as we know, that
the Asymptotic method is sensitive to the geometric form of the waterplane area
in the bow region. Hence being more blunt, than any of the reference geometries,
the Design Tool will under-estimate the wave added resistance in short wave
lengths in near-head and in head waves.
In relation to the original Design Tool, it is seen, that the modiﬁcation of the
Design Tool model have had a slightly negative inﬂuence, mainly in the high
frequency range, on the accuracy of the Design Tool.

6.4Summary of Validation and Performance
75
6.4
Summary of Validation and Performance
A performance validation of the improved Design Tool have been conducted by
analyzing nine hull geometries, whereas the results from the ﬁve most distinct
geometries have been presented.
Generally, the Design Tool under-estimates the amplitude of the peak value in
the lower frequency range. The under-estimation of the peak amplitude have
shown to be a general weakness of the linear interpolation methodology applied
in the Design Tool. To cope with this weakness, when maintaining the linear
interpolation methodology, it is believed, that a ﬁner resolution of the three
linear interpolation dimensions, being Lpp/B-ratio, B/TD-ratio, and Cb, would
reduce the discrepancy at the peak. This would result in transfer functions
laying closer together, yielding a smaller span for the linear interpolation. The
consequence of the reﬁned resolution is the rapid increase in computational eﬀort
associated with developing the databases.
The performance validation showed, that the Design Tool is sensitive to the
geometric form of the waterplane area in the bow region when predicting the
wave added resistance in the high frequency range and in bow quartering to
head waves. This yields a discrepancy in the predicted wave added resistance
for hull geometries diverging signiﬁcantly with respect to the geometric form of
the waterplane area in the bow region compared to those of the six reference
geometries.
The improved Design Tool is showing improved capability in predicting the wave
added resistance of a broad diversity of hull geometries compared to the original
Design Tool, and also with reasonable accuracy.
The comparison between the Design Tool and model test results showed good
accuracy for the DTC and KVLCC2 geometries. The results of the ULYSSES
2020 concept hull geometry showed a fair accuracy for the low speeds and
improving with increased ship speed. This for head waves and bow quartering
waves.
==PAGE:90==
76
Validation and Performance of the Improved Design Tool
==PAGE:91==
Chapter 7
The Improved Design Tool
Present chapter presents the outline of the improved Design Tool in the Excel
workbook form and as the MATLAB package. The layout of the original Design
Tool (Nielsen, 2015) is maintained with only a few changes.
7.1
Excel Design Tool
The Design Tool is implemented into an Excel workbook providing a useful and
practicable tool framing a large segment of users. The two databases is imported
into the Excel workbook, where the sequence of linear interpolation in each of
the two database are performed based on the given input parameters.
The user-interface is shown in ﬁgure 7.1, which can be split into three sections;
the input section, plot of the wave added resistance curve and wave energy
spectrum, and a plot of the wave added resistance as a function of wave heading.
Figure 7.2 shows a close-up of the input section. The main dimensions of the
ship is provided, which include the length between the perpendiculars, Lpp, the
breadth, B, the draught, T, and the block coeﬃcient, Cb. The dimensionless
parameters are computed in the program, being the length-breadth ratio, Lpp/B,
==PAGE:92==
78
The Improved Design Tool
Figure 7.1: Overview of the user interface in the Excel Design Tool.
the breadth-draught ratio, B/T, and the length-draught ratio, Lpp/T, the slen-
derness factor, Lpp/∇(1/3). The volumetric displacement, ∇, is also computed.
The limitation to the Design Tool is shown in the right-hand side with shaded
font color. It is recommended that the user comply with these limitations to
obtain a reasonable prediction of the wave added resistance. The Design Tool is
able to perform linear extrapolation of its input parameters, but as shown in
present study, this is very likely to produce inaccurate results.
Further inputs are the operational parameters in terms of wave heading relative
to the bow, Heading, and the ship speed, V s. The last is limited in a speed
range of Froude numbers equal Fn = 0 to Fn = 0.25. A violation of the speed
range triggers a warning message in the Design Tool and the user is forced to
revise the initial speed input. The maximum allowable ship speed, based on
input length, Lpp, is display with shaded on the right-hand side of input ﬁeld.
The wave energy spectrum is deﬁned in terms of signiﬁcant wave height, Hs, and
zero-crossing period, Tz, and the type of wave energy spectrum being Bretschnei-
der or JONSWAP.
The physical parameters are the water density, ρ, and the gravitational accelera-
tion, g.
The predicted wave added resistance, Rw, for the speciﬁed case is computed and
displayed at the bottom of the input section.
The plot of the wave added resistance for the analyzed scenario is shown in ﬁgure
7.3. The Design Tool predicted wave added resistance is displayed together
with the wave energy spectrum as a function of the angular wave frequency,
ω [rad/s]. The resistance curve represents the amplitude of the wave added
==PAGE:93==
7.1Excel Design Tool
79
Figure 7.2: Input section in the Excel Design Tool.
resistance normalized with the square of the incident wave amplitude, [Rw/A2].
The wave spectrum curve shows the energy of the irregular wave distributed
along the wave frequency. The more aligned the area under the two curves, the
larger the mean wave added resistance becomes.
Figure 7.3: Predicted wave added resistance and wave energy spectrum in the
Excel Design Tool.
==PAGE:94==
80
The Improved Design Tool
Figure 7.4 shows the wave added resistance as a function of the wave headings
from near-beam, β = 90◦, to head sea, β = 180◦. Recalling one of the main
assumption of the study was, that beam sea provided a zero wave added resistance.
Figure 7.4: Excel Design Tool display of the predicted wave added resistance as
a function of wave heading.
7.2
MATLAB Design Tool
The improved Design Tool is also implemented into a MATLAB package consisting
of a main script, a two functions and the two databases. The MATLAB-ﬁles and
the database ﬁles should be located in the same folder in the MATLAB directory.
The input parameters, equal to those deﬁned in section 7.1, are provided in the
main script named DesignTool.m. The main script utilizes the two functions
named getDisplayFunction.m and getWaveSpectrum, where the Design Tool
computed parameters are displayed in the MATLAB command window and the
selected wave energy spectrum is computed, respectively.
The Design Tool database consist of 18 .txt ﬁles, which the Design Tool uses
to extract the transfer functions associated with the given input values, and
performs linear interpolation between.
The Design Tool prints two ﬁgure equivalent to those in the Excel version. The
MATLAB ﬁgures are shown in ﬁgure 7.6 and 7.7 for the wave added resistance curve
and wave energy spectrum as a function of angular wave frequency, respectively.
