Chapter 8
Discussion
The physical credibility of the improved Design Tool is limited by the accuracy
of the theoretical methods applied in order to estimate the wave added resistance.
The wave added resistance problem is a non-linear quantity, where the leading
terms are of second-order. However, the radiated energy method solves the
wave added resistance problem based on linear ship motion strip theory i.e.
higher order terms are disregarded. Consequently, a linear motion estimated
with an accuracy of 10% - 15% are expected to yield a wave added resistance
with an accuracy of 20% - 30%. In addition, is the error imposed through the
interpolation sequences in the Design Tool.
The Asymptotic method used to calculate the wave added resistance in the
high frequency range was particularly sensitive to the geometric form of the
waterplane area in the bow region. This sensitivity causes a weak consistency
between the calculated wave added resistance at each wave heading. Thus, the
wave added resistance is prone to vary signiﬁcantly with just a small change of
wave heading in the range between bow quartering and head sea. Moreover, the
Asymptotic method assumes the ship side to be a vertical wall at the free surface.
This assumption disregards the eﬀect from large bow ﬂare, which is common for
container ships and RoRo ships, on the wave added resistance around head seas.
The literature (Seo et al., 2013) supports, that the Asymptotic method performs
well for hull geometries with a blunt bow region, whereas less favourable for
slender hull forms. It most therefore be expected, that the improved Design Tool
==PAGE:98==
84
Discussion
predicts the wave added resistance in a natural seaway of short wave lengths for
slender hull geometries with limited degree of accuracy.
The averaging process showed, that the transfer functions for the wave added
resistance, calculated by the radiated energy method, provided un-physically high
results for a number of scaling combinations, and especially for hull geometries
with a low block coeﬃcient and at low forward ship speed. The behavior was
shown to be consistent for hull geometries with a Lpp/TD-ratio exceeding 20. The
hydrodynamic coeﬃcients and the RAO functions for heave and pitch motion
has converged, and on is inclined to believe, that the source to this behavior is
hidden in the radiated energy method. It is outside the scope of present study
to perform a methodical study striving to identify the source, but the Design
Tool user should be conscious about the physical credibility of the tool when
analyzing hull geometries with high Lpp/TD-ratio at low forward ship speed.
The performance validation of the original Design Tool showed a distinct dis-
crepancy in the prediction of wave added resistance for hull geometries with
Lpp/B-ratio and B/TD-ratio outside the range bracketing the database i.e. using
linear extrapolating to obtain the result. However, fairly good results were seen
for ratios inside the limitations, but with block coeﬃcients signiﬁcantly lower
than the range spanned by the ﬁve reference geometries. The improved Design
Tool is based on the same methodology, it is reasonable to assumed, that wave
added resistance predicted by small linear extrapolation is within acceptable
accuracy. No comprehensive study of the improved Design Tool’s ability to
predict the wave added resistance with an acceptable accuracy, when linearly
extrapolating, has been conducted as such hull geometry was not available.
Thus, the limitations of the improved Design Tool, with regards to Lpp/B-ratio,
B/TD-ratio, and block coeﬃcient, should be respected by the user.
As expected, the Strip theory method showed un-physical results in terms of a
signiﬁcant over-estimation of the wave added resistance at zero forward speed
calculations. WAMIT results for the wave added resistance at zero forward speed
is implemented into the improved Design Tool, yielding an increased accuracy
for zero forward speed.
Validation of the improved Design Tool using eight diﬀerent hull geometries
showed generally good agreements with the direct calculations in the considered
frequency range. The largest deviation occurs around the peak values, which are
generally under-estimated by the improved Design Tool. The linear interpolation
results in an under-estimation of the peak values at each interpolation sequence.
The general deviation of the peak value was around 30-35%, which is considered
acceptable.
Comparison between the improved Design Tool and model test results showed a
good coherence for the KVLCC2 and DTC at high and low forward ship speed
==PAGE:99==
85
in head waves, respectively. The ULYSSES results showed reasonable coherence
at low forward ship speeds with increased accuracy with increased forward ship
speed. This for head waves and bow quartering waves.
==PAGE:100==
86
Discussion
==PAGE:101==
Chapter 9
Conclusion and
Recommendations
9.1
Conclusion
The main objective of this study has been to improve the original Design Tool
(Nielsen, 2015).
This have been achieved by validating the applicability of
the original Design Tool by comparing transfer functions for the wave added
resistance predicted by the tool and the direct calculations for eight diﬀerent hull
geometries. The original Design Tool showed good predicts for geometries not
violating the Lpp/B-ratio and B/TD-ratio limitations. Inaccurate predictions
was seen for geometries with Lpp/B-ratio and B/TD-ratio outside the limitations
i.e. the Design Tool is linearly extrapolating to predict the wave added resistance.
The improvements implemented into the Design Tool include expressing the
speed in terms of the Froude number and with increased resolution (Fn =
0.00, 0.05, 0.10, ..., 0.25).
The Lpp/B-ratio (4 ≤Lpp/B ≤8) and B/T-ratio
(2 ≤B/T ≤5) have been expanded, and the block coeﬃcient is introduced
as an additional input parameter. The improved Design Tool now becomes a
function of the Froude number, wave headings, Lpp/B-ratio, B/T-ratio, and
block coeﬃcient. Moreover, WAMIT calculation of the wave added resistance for
zero forward ship speed is implemented into the Design Tool, which introduces a
signiﬁcant increase in accuracy at near zero speed.
==PAGE:102==
88
Conclusion and Recommendations
The improved Design Tool predictions have been compared with direct calcula-
tions of the wave added resistance transfer functions from eight diﬀerent hull
geometries, which generally showed a very good agreement and also an improved
accuracy compared to the original Design Tool.
Moreover, the improved Design Tool was compared with model test results for
the DTC container ship type hull, KVLCC2 tanker type hull, and the ULYSSES
2020 tanker/ bulker type hull, which all showed reasonably good accuracy for
the low speed case, and increased accuracy with increased ship speed.
The Design Tool has been successfully implemented into an Excel workbook
and a MATLAB package, which is publicly available for naval architects and rule
makers as a useful tool for prediction of wave added resistance or as part of the
evaluation process for how to best satisfy environmental requirements, while
ensuring safe operation of the ship.
9.2
Recommendation for Future Work
The improved Design Tool provides a quick estimate of the wave added resistance,
with reasonable accuracy, providing only the main dimensions and the block
coeﬃcient for a large diversity of ship hull geometries. The recommendations
for future work focus on improving the physical credibility of the tool. The
improvements include:
• Loading conditions as input. General cargo ships are commonly operated
at loading conditions diﬀerent from its design conditions, due to large
variety in the weight of the cargo loaded onboard. This typically yields an
aft trim and smaller draughts, which increases the sensitive towards wave
added resistance. Due to this, the estimation of the wave added resistance
of ship in ballast or half full loaded conditions increases the applicability
of the tool.
• Sway forces and yaw moments for naval architects to predict maneuver-
ability and rudder size.
• Other theoretical method for the wave added resistance in short wave
lengths e.g the momentum conservation method developed by Maruo.
• Implement a corrections method to the radiated energy method for low
forward ship speeds.
• All validation of the Design Tool have been performed by comparing the
dimensionless wave added resistance for regular waves. An identiﬁcation the
==PAGE:103==
9.2Recommendation for Future Work
89
inﬂuence of the discrepancy between the wave added resistance predicted
by the Design Tool and direct calculations in irregular waves would be
interesting.
• Add other wave energy spectrum to Design Tool.
Recommendation for a future re-construction of the Design Tool based on the
methodology presented in this study, would include:
• The resolution of the Lpp/B-ratio should not exceed a step size of Lpp/B =
2.0, and preferably less. The resolution of the B/T-ratio should not exceed
B/T ≤1.5.
• A ﬁner resolution of the block coeﬃcient to avoid a large span for the linear
interpolation.

==PAGE:119==
Appendix B
Ship Data
Present appendix contains ship data for the 6 reference geometries, and each of
the 9 scaling combinations. These are ship speciﬁc data used as input to the
I-ship software for computing ship motions and hydrodynamic coeﬃcients. The
6 reference geometries are Torm Lilly, the Bulk Carrier, Seahorse 35, 71m RoPax,
Container Ship, and the HTC container ship.

Appendix C
Validation of Linear
Interpolation in the Original
Design Tool
Present appendix contains a validation of the linear interpolation process done in
the original Design Tool. The validation is done by comparing transfer functions
from Design Tool and by direct calculation of the un-scaled hull geometries.
Note, that the legend is located on the last ﬁgure out of 9 (lower tight-hand side
corner). The results from 2 out of 5 representative hull geometries are presented
in this appendix to constrain the number of pages.

==PAGE:135==
Appendix D
Performance Validation of
the Original Design Tool
A performance validation of the original Design Tool has been conducted by
analysis of 9 diﬀerent hull geometries, whereas 5 hull geometries are presented
in section 4.2, and the 4 remaining hull geometries are presented in present
appendix.
D.1
Analysis of Hamburg Test Case
The Hamburg Test Case (HTC) is a modern container ship with Cb = 0.65,
which is widely use in academical studies, thus various publication of model test
and CFD results are easily accessible. The HTC is a single-screw container ship
with a bulbous bow, a slender midship section and a nearly non-separating ﬂow
at the stern, and a fair representation of a realistic modern container hull. The
main particulars of the HTC hull appears in table D.1.
==PAGE:136==

Performance Validation of the Original Design Tool

D.1.1
Geometry
The geometry is described in ﬁgure D.1 with 31 station curves with an increased
density in the aft and fore part of the hull.
Figure D.1: Geometry representation of the Hamburg Test Case container ship.
D.1.2
Wave Added Resistance
The hull geometry of the HTC container ship is within the Lpp/B-ratio and
B/T-ratio bracketing the database, though the Cb = 0.64 is fairly less, than the
range spanned by the reference geometries.
==PAGE:137==
D.1Analysis of Hamburg Test Case
123
The Design Tool and direct calculation aligns well for ship speeds less than
Fn = 0.1 and near-beam wave headings. An over-estimation is seen in the high
frequency for bow quartering to head waves, and the tendency is ampliﬁed for
increased ship speed. A small phase shift between the peak values in the low
frequency range is seen, and generally, the Design Tool under-estimates peak
value.
Figure D.2 shows a series of transfer functions, and the described tendency.
Comparison between a limited number of headings and ship speeds appears in
Appendix E.

D.2.1
Geometry
The geometry is described in ﬁgure D.3 with 13 station curves and a slight
increase in density in the bow and stern part of the hull. The available body
plans for present hull geometry was limited in detail, thus the low number of
station curves.
==PAGE:139==
D.2Analysis of Container ship
125
Figure D.3: Geometry representation of the Container Ship
D.2.2
Wave Added Resistance
The Container Ship lies inside the Lpp/B-ratio and B/T-ratio bracketing the
database, and the Cb = 0.52 is signiﬁcantly less than the range spanned by the 5
reference geometries. The geometry is particular due to the very slender bow
form with small gradients in the longitudinal direction compared to the reference
geometries.
Generally, a good coherence is seen in the high frequencies for beam and bow
quartering wave headings (β = 100◦to β = 140◦). For headings from bow
quartering to head sea an over-estimation evolves, which is ampliﬁed with
increased ship speeds. The largest discrepancy is seen in head sea at highest
ship speed.
The Design Tool under-estimates the peak value with up to 30% and with a
phase shift, so that the peak appears at a lower frequency i.e. longer wavelengths.
Figure D.4 shows the tendency in a series of headings and ship speeds. Results
for a limited number of headings and ship speeds appears in Appendix E.

D.3
Analysis of 126m RoPax
The 126m RoPax is a twin-screw roll-on roll-oﬀ2.000 passenger capacity car
ferry designed to operate within the Baltic Sea. It has a bulbous bow, skeg, and
an extreme U-shape in the aft part. The main particulars appears in table D.3.
D.3.1
Geometry
The hull geometry is described in ﬁgure D.5 by 23 station curves with an increased
density in the aft and fore part of the hull.
D.3.2
Wave Added Resistance
The hull geometry lays outside the upper value for B/T-ratio yielding extrapola-
tion, and the Cb = 0.64 is somewhat less than the reference geometries.
==PAGE:141==

Generally, the Design Tool estimates the peak value at a lower frequency than
the direct calculation, where the discrepancy is reduced with increased forward
speed.
Consistently with the 109m RoPax ferry, a fairly good coherence is seen in the
high frequency range for wave headings from β = 110◦to β = 140◦for ship
speed higher than Fn = 0.10. The hull geometry shares the same characteristics
as the 109m RoPax ferry, thus similar tendencies is seen in comparison between
the two transfer functions.
Figure D.6 shows described tendencies for the 126m RoPax ferry. Comparison
of a limited number of headings and ship speeds appears in Appendix E.

Figure D.6: Comparison of estimated wave added resistance transfer functions

D.4
Analysis of 71m RoPax
The 71m RoPax Ship is a twin-screw 388 passenger capacity ferry operating
between smaller islands and the main land of Iceland. The 71m RoPax Ship
has a bulbous bow, an extreme U-shape at the aft part, and constitute one of
the 3 ship at the extremity of B/T ratio. The main particulars appears in table
D.4. The hull geometry is particular with the low value of Lpp/B-ratio and high
B/T-ratio i.e. a hull geometry with a large breadth compared to the ship length,
and a large breadth compared to the draught. Moreover, this geometry is shorter
then the other analyzed geometries.
D.4.1
Geometry
The geometry is described in ﬁgure D.7 by 24 station curves with increased
density in the aft and fore part.

D.4.2
Wave Added Resistance
The 71m RoPax geometry lays outside the lower value of Lpp/B-ratio and upper
value of B/T-ratio bracketing the database, moreover, the Cb = 0.58 is signiﬁcant
less than the range spanned by the reference geometries.
A fairly large discrepancy between the two curves is seen for the low frequency
range, where an oscillatory behavior evolves with increased ship speed. This
behavior is ampliﬁed with wave headings approaching head waves.
A reasonable coherence is seen for near-beam to bow quartering wave headings

Appendix E
Validation of the Original
Design Tool Performance
Present appendix contains a validation of the performance of the original Design
Tool. The validation is done by comparing transfer functions from the Design
Tool and direct calculations of a series of hull geometries diverging from the ﬁve
hull geometries constituting the basis for the Design Tool database. The hull
geometries are presented in A.

Validation of the Original Design Tool Performance
==PAGE:171==
Appendix F
Salvesen’s Radiated Energy
Method
Present appendix concerns the averaging process of the 6 hull geometries scaled
to the 9 scaling ratios (S1, S2, ..., S9), where an adverse behavior of the com-
puted wave added resistance transfer function by the radiated energy method
by (Salvesen, 1978) is observed for geometries with high a Lpp/T-ratio. The
presentation of the averaging process is limited to three wave headings including
all 9 combinations of scaling ratios and forward ship speeds. This is to limited
the size of appendix.

==PAGE:181==
Appendix G
Validation of Linear
Interpolation in the
Improved Design Tool
Present appendix contains a validation of the linear interpolation process done
in the improved Design Tool database. The validation is done by comparing
transfer functions from Design Tool and direct calculations of the un-scaled hull
geometries. Note, that the legend is located on the ﬁrst ﬁgure out of 9 (upper-left
corner).

180
Validation of Linear Interpolation in the Improved Design Tool
==PAGE:195==
Appendix H
Performance Validation of
the Improved Design Tool
Present appendix contains a validation of the performance of the improved
Design Tool. Results are shown for wave headings β = 100◦, β = 100◦, and
β = 100◦to limit the number of pages. Besides the hull geometries presented in
the report a few more hulls have been utilized for validating the performance
of the improved Design Tool. The particulars of the hull geometries, which are
additional to those presented in the report is presented in chapter 4.

Appendix I
Model Test Validation of
Improved Design Tool
Present appendix contains the additional results on the comparison between the
original and improved Design Tool predictions and model test results. Results
are shown for the ULYSSES 2020 concept tanker hull for head waves (β = 180◦)
and quartering wave (β = 150◦) at ship speed U = 10knots.
